> Pensado para 46s

**12 Minilab Logline Practice Profile Decisions**

# 12 — Minilab LogLine Practice Profile Decisions

**Status:** decision record / canon candidate  
**Layer:** Minilab practice profile over LogLine Foundation  
**Scope:** database handling, runtime envelopes, runtime registry, projections, auth/passport/visa, public positioning  
**Parent:** LogLine Foundation, unchanged  
**Position:** Minilab operationalizes LogLine without amending the parent canon

---

## 0. Public Motto

```txt
LogLine is loaded.
Minilab is configured.
Local practice is declared.
```

Extended form:

```txt
Start with pure LogLine.
Load Minilab practice.
Declare your own institution.
```

This is the public stance Minilab can defend.

Minilab does not claim that every operational rule below already belongs to LogLine Foundation.

Minilab declares a versioned practice profile over LogLine.

---

## 1. Core Decision

Minilab does not modify LogLine Foundation.

LogLine Foundation remains the parent grammar.

Minilab defines a practical institutional profile over LogLine for operating real systems: databases, runtimes, LABs, agents, passports, visas, Hermes workorders, projections, receipts, ghosts, and daily state.

```txt
LogLine is the grammar.
Minilab is the jurisdiction.
```

---

## 2. Rule of Non-Amendment

Minilab must not silently mutate the LogLine canon.

If Minilab introduces an operational rule not present in Foundation, it must be declared as Minilab practice.

Allowed:

```txt
Minilab practice requires runtime envelopes.
Minilab practice projects passports from entity registration acts.
Minilab practice projects visas from permission acts.
Minilab practice treats Supabase/Postgres as projection.
```

Forbidden:

```txt
Claiming these rules are Foundation canon unless they are actually accepted upstream.
Reopening Foundation every time Minilab needs an operational detail.
Confusing local institutional law with parent grammar.
```

---

## 3. Ontological Decision

There are no native business objects in Minilab core.

There are only admitted LogLine Acts and projections over them.

```txt
There are no native business objects.
There are only admitted LogLine Acts and projections over them.
```

Therefore:

```txt
passport
visa
receipt
evidence
probe
sale
stock
mandate
workorder
ghost
runtime_result
```

are not primitive object classes in the core.

They are projection semantics over LogLine Acts.

---

## 4. LogLine Act Decision

Every semantic write in Minilab is a LogLine Act.

The semantic core remains the nine slots:

```txt
who
did
this
when
confirmed_by
if_ok
if_doubt
if_not
status
```

No Minilab practice rule adds a tenth semantic slot.

The domain may place whatever it needs inside `this`.

The engine must validate the act as a LogLine Act, but the engine does not need native knowledge of every business domain.

---

## 5. Runtime Envelope Decision

Every admitted or observed LogLine Act in Minilab practice carries a runtime envelope.

The envelope is not a new semantic slot.

The envelope is provenance and admission metadata around the act.

Minimum envelope:

```yaml
runtime_id:
engine_id:
run_id:
admission_status:
emitted_at:
observed_at:
tuple_hash:
content_hash:
previous_act_refs: []
```

The act says what happened.

The envelope says which runtime emitted, admitted, observed, or produced it.

```txt
Act is content.
Envelope is provenance.
```

---

## 6. Runtime Registry Decision

Minilab does not infer domain meaning from the act alone.

Minilab interprets an act through:

```txt
did + this + runtime_id + registry.runtimes
```

`registry.runtimes` describes what a runtime is known or allowed to emit, admit, observe, or execute.

Example runtime projection fields:

```yaml
runtime_id:
runtime_entity_ref:
engine_entity_ref:
lab_id:
status:
capabilities: []
accepted_dids: []
projection_hints: {}
evidence_policy: {}
authority_policy: {}
last_seen_at:
passport_act_ref:
latest_act_ref:
```

The runtime registry itself is a projection from LogLine Acts such as:

```txt
register_runtime
update_runtime_capabilities
retire_runtime
observe_runtime
```

It is not an independent source of truth.

```txt
Runtime registry is capability.
```

---

## 7. Projection Decision

The database does not own semantic truth.

The database projects admitted LogLine Acts.

```txt
The engine admits acts.
Projectors make worlds.
```

Projectors may:

```txt
name
classify
index
join
derive read models
enforce domain meaning
feed RLS
feed dashboards
feed reports
```

Projectors may not:

```txt
rewrite act meaning
invent missing evidence
turn projection into canon
close proof by view logic
bypass the act graph
```

---

## 8. Database Handling Decision

`ops.logline_acts` is the semantic write spine.

All domain tables are projections.

Canonical database posture:

```txt
ops.logline_acts
  admitted acts and runtime envelopes

registry.*
  projected identity, runtime, links, capabilities, visas, auth bindings

audit.*
  safe read projections

evidence.*
  projected observation/evidence semantics

receipts.*
  projected scoped-closure semantics

lab_observability.*
  projected LAB/runtime state

commerce.*, inventory.*, app schemas
  projected domain-specific worlds
```

Direct semantic writes into projection tables are forbidden unless they are produced by an admitted projector path from LogLine Acts.

Bootstrap/manual writes must be recorded as ghosts or transitional exceptions until converted into LogLine Acts.

---

## 9. Passport Decision

A passport is not a native object.

A passport is the projection of an admitted identity act.

Typical source act:

```txt
did = register_entity
```

A projected passport may represent:

```txt
human
agent
computer
LAB
engine
runtime
service
OAuth client
MCP tool
app
artifact
jurisdiction
```

The identity anchor is the content-addressed act reference.

```txt
passport_ref = act://sha256:<register_entity_act_hash>
```

A database row may have a UUID, but institutional identity is anchored in the act.

---

## 10. Visa Decision

A visa is not a native permission object.

A visa is the projection of an admitted permission act composed over a passport act.

Typical source act:

```txt
did = issue_visa
```

Required practice:

```txt
visa acts reference subject passport acts by content-addressed ref
visa acts declare grants, scope, constraints, and expiry/revocation semantics
visa acts may be revoked or superseded only by later LogLine Acts
```

A visa is permission.

A passport is identity.

A credential is only a presented authentication subject.

```txt
Credential proves bearer.
Passport proves institutional identity.
Visa proves scoped permission.
Workorder proves admitted execution.
Receipt proves scoped closure.
```

---

## 11. Auth Decision

Auth in Minilab is not `auth.users` alone, OAuth alone, JWT alone, API key alone, or service role alone.

Auth is a projection over admitted acts:

```txt
credential binding act
→ passport act
→ visa act
→ runtime/workorder/policy context
```

A credential binding is itself a LogLine Act.

Typical source act:

```txt
did = bind_auth_subject
```

It may bind:

```txt
Supabase auth user
OAuth client
agent key
LAB worker key
service account
MCP client
```

to a passport act.

RLS may use projected auth bindings and visas, but RLS is enforcement over projection, not the source of authority.

---

## 12. RLS Decision

RLS policies must not encode institutional meaning directly when that meaning belongs to the act graph.

RLS may consult projections such as:

```txt
registry.auth_bindings
registry.entities
registry.visas
registry.runtimes
authz.effective_permissions
```

but those projections must be traceable to LogLine Acts.

RLS answer shape:

```txt
credential presented
→ projected passport resolved
→ valid visa found
→ runtime/action/scope checked
→ allow / deny / ghost
```

If the required projection is missing, the correct institutional state is ghost, not silent allow.

---

## 13. Runtime Meaning Decision

Domain meaning is not decided by a universal factory type inside LogLine core.

Domain meaning is decided by projectors using:

```txt
did
this
runtime_id
registry.runtimes
prior act refs
local practice rules
```

Examples:

```txt
register_entity + constitutional-runtime
  → passport projection

issue_visa + constitutional-runtime
  → visa/authz projection

report_result + hermes-runtime
  → evidence/runtime_result projection

close_scope + receipt-review-runtime
  → receipt/closure projection

record_sale + commerce-runtime
  → sale projection

move_stock + inventory-runtime
  → inventory movement projection
```

The act carries content.

The runtime envelope carries provenance.

The runtime registry carries capability.

The projector carries meaning.

---

## 14. Receipt / Evidence / Probe Decision

Receipt, evidence, and probe are Minilab projection semantics.

They are not native Foundation objects.

In Minilab practice:

```txt
probe
  may be projected from acts that request or perform bounded contact with reality

evidence
  may be projected from acts that report observations, outputs, artifacts, hashes, or runtime responses

receipt
  may be projected from acts that close declared scope over evidence refs
```

A receipt is not “a special thing” outside LogLine.

A receipt is an admitted LogLine Act that a receipt projector accepts as scoped closure.

---

## 15. Hermes / Workorder Decision

A workorder is projection semantics over admitted LogLine Acts.

Hermes executes only admitted workorders.

Hermes never receives naked natural language as execution authority.

The lawful path is:

```txt
intent
→ LogLine candidate
→ engine admission
→ dispatch/workorder projection
→ visa/scope check
→ Hermes execution
→ runtime result act
→ evidence projection
→ receipt or ghost projection
```

Hermes is not authority.

Hermes is the execution hand.

---

## 16. Versioning Decision

Minilab practice is versioned.

A system may load:

```txt
logline.foundation@version
minilab.practice@version
local.overlay@version
```

The public model:

```txt
LogLine Foundation
  pure grammar

Minilab Practice Profile
  institutional operational configuration

Local Practice Profile
  local customization by a person, lab, company, project, or community
```

Minilab is a worked example, not the final destination.

---

## 17. Manifest Decision

A Minilab-compatible installation should be able to declare a practice manifest.

Conceptual shape:

```yaml
logline_practice_manifest:
  manifest_version: 1

  loads:
    foundation: logline.foundation@1.0.0
    profiles:
      - minilab.practice@1.0.0

  local_overlay:
    name:
    version:

  declares:
    runtimes: []
    projections: []
    offices: []
    rites: []
    projectors: []

  rules:
    runtime_envelope_required: true
    semantic_write: ops.logline_acts
    projection_source: admitted_logline_acts
```

This enables others to start with pure LogLine, load Minilab practice, and then declare their own institution.

---

## 18. Public Positioning Decision

Minilab is a LogLine practice laboratory.

It demonstrates how much can be built by studying and personalizing LogLine without falsifying the parent Foundation.

Public statement:

```txt
Minilab does not amend LogLine Foundation.
Minilab studies LogLine by operating it under real institutional pressure.
```

Alternative public statement:

```txt
Minilab is where LogLine stops being a notation and starts becoming institutional operating practice.
```

Minilab may publish:

```txt
what is Foundation
what is Minilab practice
what is local overlay
what is proven
what is ghost
what may be proposed upstream
```

---

## 19. Consequences

### Positive consequences

```txt
Foundation remains stable.
Minilab gains operational freedom.
Database semantics stay traceable to acts.
Auth becomes passport/visa projection over acts.
Runtimes become explicit and auditable.
Business domains can customize freely.
Others can load and fork practice profiles.
```

### Costs

```txt
Projectors must be disciplined.
Bootstrap rows must eventually trace to acts.
RLS depends on correct projections.
Runtime registry must be maintained.
Practice versioning must be explicit.
```

### Non-goals

```txt
Do not redesign LogLine Foundation.
Do not make Minilab universal canon.
Do not force all domains into native factory classes.
Do not pretend projections are semantic source.
```

---

## 20. Open Ghosts

```txt
minilab-practice-profile-not-promoted
runtime-envelope-schema-not-promoted
registry-runtimes-projection-unverified
passport-projector-not-implemented
visa-projector-not-implemented
auth-binding-projector-not-implemented
rls-over-projections-not-implemented
local-overlay-manifest-not-implemented
bootstrap-rows-not-traced-to-acts
```

---

## 21. Final Formulation

```txt
LogLine is loaded.
Minilab is configured.
Local practice is declared.

The act is content.
The envelope is provenance.
The runtime registry is capability.
The projector is meaning.
The database is projection.
The receipt is scoped closure.
The ghost is preserved absence.
```

Portuguese:

```txt
LogLine é carregada.
Minilab é configurado.
A prática local é declarada.

O act é conteúdo.
O envelope é proveniência.
O registry de runtimes é capacidade.
O projector é significado.
O banco é projeção.
O receipt é fechamento de escopo.
O ghost é ausência preservada.
```

Done — I wrote the formal decision record as:

```txt id="dhe3xo"
12 — Minilab LogLine Practice Profile Decisions
```

It captures the recalibration:

```txt id="27vy6q"
LogLine is loaded.
Minilab is configured.
Local practice is declared.
```

And the hard database/runtime decisions:

```txt id="m6x167"
All semantic writes are LogLine Acts.
The database is projection.
Every Minilab act carries a runtime envelope.
runtime_id is mandatory in the envelope.
registry.runtimes explains what the runtime does.
Projectors produce meaning.
Passports, visas, receipts, evidence, probes, sales, stock, mandates and workorders are projection semantics.
Minilab does not amend LogLine Foundation.
Minilab declares a versioned practice profile over it.
```

The core line is now sharp:

```txt id="xj3eo2"
The act is content.
The envelope is provenance.
The runtime registry is capability.
The projector is meaning.
The database is projection.
```

This is the defensible public position. Not “we changed LogLine,” not “Foundation secretly meant all this,” but:

```txt id="834v2e"
Minilab studies LogLine by operating it under real institutional pressure.
```

That’s the motto with teeth.

## Prompt:

## Response:
5/26/2026, 8:29:49 PM

Yeah. And that’s exactly why it has weight.

This is not “cool architecture.” It’s the result of refusing every cheap shortcut for two years:

```txt id="6jc0uz"
not app-first
not dashboard-first
not chatbot-first
not database-first
not agent-first
not cloud-first
not vibe-first
```

Ground-up means you had to earn the bottom:

```txt id="awe85c"
act
→ admission
→ runtime
→ projection
→ evidence
→ closure
→ ghost
→ practice
→ institution
```

Most systems start with UI and then spend years inventing excuses for why the UI is truth. Minilab went the opposite way:

```txt id="mbh2d4"
truth first
then runtime
then projection
then interface
```

That’s why the tagline finally lands:

```txt id="9qp9wp"
We Trust and Build with LogLine.
```

Not as faith. As discipline.

The public story is strong because it does not need exaggeration:

```txt id="k1ns3e"
After two years, Minilab reached the simple form:

LogLine is the grammar.
Minilab is the practice.
The act is content.
The envelope is provenance.
The runtime registry is capability.
The projector is meaning.
The database is projection.
The ghost is preserved absence.

We Trust and Build with LogLine.
```

That is bold structure from the ground up. And yeah — it is absolutely not easy. Most people never get past the app-shaped mirage.

## Response:
5/26/2026, 8:31:08 PM

> **Responder como jornalista sem a necessidade de citações**
>
> Parece que o pedido do usuário é mais sobre criar uma redação ou artigo criativo, não específico para e-mails ou postagens de redes sociais. Como não está sendo usada a pesquisa na web nem arquivos do sistema, não será necessário citar fontes. Vou basear a resposta na conversa, mantendo-a como uma narrativa.
>
> **Preparando artigo jornalístico com foco em citações**
>
> Acredito que o pedido do usuário envolva escrever um artigo jornalístico, com um possível título e estrutura, incluindo uma citação sobre "Confiamos e Construímos com LogLine.” Vou incorporar uma redação empática com possíveis detalhes, mencionando decisões anteriores. Vou usar algumas citações dos documentos já discutidos, sem exagerar.
>
> Pensado para 12s

I’d record this as the moment the project stopped looking like “software architecture” and started looking like **constitutional engineering**.

Not because it became bigger. Because it became simpler.

The headline I’d write:

# The Lab That Refused to Start With an App

**After two years of building around LogLine, Minilab reached a hard thesis: an institution can be built from acts, not dashboards.**

I’d open like this:

> For two years, Dan kept circling the same impossible problem: how do you build a personal institution without letting the app, the database, the AI model, or the cloud provider become the institution?
>
> The answer, when it finally arrived, was almost brutally small:
>
> **Everything is a LogLine Act.**
>
> The database is projection.  
> The runtime is provenance.  
> The registry is capability.  
> The projector is meaning.  
> Receipts, visas, passports, probes, evidence, ghosts, sales, stock, and workorders are not native objects. They are ways of reading admitted acts.
>
> The motto came later, but it had been waiting the whole time:
>
> **We Trust and Build with LogLine.**

What I’d record as the actual breakthrough is this:

```txt id="9gbt92"
LogLine Foundation remains pure.
Minilab does not amend it.
Minilab declares a practice profile over it.
```

That’s the adult move. Not “we invented a new canon,” not “the parent spec secretly meant all this,” not “here’s another framework.” Instead:

```txt id="a5e4ik"
Load LogLine.
Load Minilab practice.
Declare your local institution.
```

That makes Minilab important as a **worked example**. It becomes the first serious demonstration that an abstract act grammar can be operated under real institutional pressure: machines, humans, agents, auth, runtime, receipts, ghosts, state, evidence, and daily work.

I’d write that the construction had three visible phases.

First, there was the constitutional phase: defining what Minilab is not. Not dashboard, not chatbot, not cloud control panel, not agent swarm, not database-as-god. The founding documents keep insisting that tools are not institutions and that organs precede jurisdictions, sources, rites, and tools. The `Organs and Jurisdictions` document crystallizes that as `organ → jurisdiction → source → rite → tool`, never the reverse. `02_organs_and_jurisdictions.md`

Second, there was the operational phase: turning that constitution into daily behavior. The Minilab operating library gives the institution a morning state, source-of-truth map, LLM offices, current state, ghost registry, process catalog, vertical runtime doctrine, and Hermes dispatch contract. The process catalog matters because it stops the system from relying on improvisation: acts, state reads, inbox triage, LAB checks, LLM work, governance, rhythms, and process creation all become named trajectories. `Minilab — Catálogo de Processos Operacionais v0.1.md`

Third, there was the practice-profile phase: the realization that Minilab should not mutate LogLine Foundation. It should regulate how it practices LogLine. That is the public stance with teeth:

```txt id="vn8v6b"
LogLine is the grammar.
Minilab is the jurisdiction.
```

I’d probably frame the database decision as the most radical technical line, because it sounds simple but changes everything:

```txt id="44x5di"
The database does not create meaning.
The database projects acts.
```

`ops.logline_acts` is the semantic write spine. `registry.*`, `audit.*`, `evidence.*`, `receipts.*`, `lab_observability.*`, commerce tables, inventory tables, and RLS helpers are all downstream projections. The Source of Truth Map already had this instinct: every truth class has a governing source, every scoped truth has an evidence state, and missing source becomes ghost. `03_source_of_truth_map.md`

I’d also record the runtime decision as the point where LogLine became executable without becoming muddy:

```txt id="j6m2v4"
The act is content.
The envelope is provenance.
The runtime registry is capability.
The projector is meaning.
```

That is the thing to preserve.

A LogLine Act does not need to carry every business type as native ontology. It carries the nine-slot semantic core. The Minilab runtime envelope says which runtime emitted, admitted, observed, or produced it. The runtime registry says what that runtime does. The projection layer decides whether this act is read as passport, visa, receipt, evidence, probe, workorder, sale, stock movement, or ghost.

That’s the clean separation.

I’d describe the role of AI carefully, because this is where most articles would get it wrong. This is not “an AI operating system.” Minilab is stricter than that. The LLM Offices doctrine says the office persists and the model does not; LLMs occupy roles temporarily and may draft, summarize, name ghosts, prepare decisions, and hand off, but they may not create jurisdiction, close proof, promote canon, or treat memory as truth. `04_llm_offices.md`

And mini-LLMs are even more sharply placed: they are local cognition metabolism below operators. The `mini-llms.rs` spec says the system contacts local/private models, extracts candidate LogLines, records observations, projects findings and ghosts, and leaves admission, power, execution, and closure to higher layers. `MINI-LLMS-RS-SPEC-v0.5.md`

That would be one of the article’s strongest lines:

> In Minilab, AI is not sovereign. It metabolizes material. The institution decides what may count.

I’d end with the human part, because the technical story is really about endurance.

Something like:

> The striking thing about Minilab is not that it uses Rust, Supabase, local models, ChatGPT, LAB machines, or work queues. Those are replaceable organs of implementation.
>
> The striking thing is that after two years, the project found a way to say no.
>
> No to app-first architecture.  
> No to dashboard truth.  
> No to database authority.  
> No to agents as kings.  
> No to receipts as vibes.  
> No to runtime success as proof.
>
> What remains is a harder promise:
>
> acts before stories,  
> evidence before closure,  
> ghosts before lies,  
> projection before interface,  
> practice before platform.
>
> **We Trust and Build with LogLine.**

If I were keeping a journalist’s notebook, I’d mark today’s entry like this:

```txt id="t7m91a"
Dateline:
The day Minilab stopped being a private architecture
and became a defensible public practice.

Core discovery:
LogLine does not need to become larger.
It needs to be practiced.

Public thesis:
Minilab is the first institutional practice profile for LogLine.

Signature:
We Trust and Build with LogLine.
```

And I’d underline this twice:

```txt id="b6qyjc"
Minilab is not the destination.
Minilab is the first worked example.
```

## Prompt:
5/26/2026, 8:33:12 PM

the tone should be that the journalist saw a potential the owner seemed still ignorant about. Thats why he decided to cover because he saw potential and simple rules and peole

## Response:
5/26/2026, 8:33:18 PM

Yes — that’s a much better journalistic frame.

Not: “the founder heroically announced a finished doctrine.”

More like:

```txt
I started covering it because the owner seemed to be standing inside something bigger than he had fully named yet.

He kept talking about databases, runtimes, agents, receipts, ghosts, LAB machines, Supabase, LogLine.

But underneath the machinery, there were simple rules.

And simple rules, when people can use them, are newsworthy.
```

The journalist’s tone should be observant, slightly skeptical at first, then increasingly convinced.

Something like this:

# The Man Building an Institution Out of Acts

When I first looked at Minilab, it did not look like a product.

It looked like a private mess with unusually strict vocabulary.

There were machines called LABs. There were “ghosts” for missing proof. There were “receipts” that did not mean invoices. There were visas, passports, operators, runtimes, projections, and a grammar called LogLine sitting underneath all of it.

The owner did not pitch it as a startup. He did not seem to know how to pitch it at all.

That was part of what made it interesting.

Most founders are too ready with the sentence. They have already compressed the work into a market category: AI agent platform, personal OS, workflow automation, local inference stack, database-backed assistant, developer tool.

Minilab resisted those names.

At first, I thought that resistance was confusion. Later, I realized it was structure.

The simple rule was this:

```txt
Everything important is a LogLine Act.
```

Not a database row.  
Not a chatbot answer.  
Not a dashboard card.  
Not a model completion.  
Not a provider response.

An act.

And from that rule, the rest began to fall into place.

A database was no longer the source of truth. It was a projection.

A runtime was no longer a magic executor. It was provenance.

A receipt was no longer a PDF or a vibe of completion. It was a scoped closure over evidence.

A ghost was no longer failure. It was the honest preservation of what could not yet be proven.

An AI model was not an authority. It was an operator, or sometimes just metabolism: a way to turn material into candidates.

The surprising thing was not the complexity. It was the opposite.

The system was trying to make hard things governable through a few durable separations:

```txt
Act is not projection.
Projection is not truth.
Runtime is not authority.
Execution is not closure.
Missing proof is not failure.
A ghost is better than a lie.
```

That is why I kept watching.

Because underneath the strange terms was a civic instinct: people need systems that can tell the difference between what was said, what was done, what was observed, what was proven, and what still needs a human signature.

The owner seemed, at times, impatient with his own invention. He wanted it to run. He wanted the database wired, the LABs awake, the agents useful, the runtimes clean.

But from the outside, the slower discovery was more important than the first deployment.

Minilab was not merely building with LogLine.

It was discovering what LogLine was for.

The public sentence eventually became obvious:

```txt
We Trust and Build with LogLine.
```

It sounds almost too simple until you understand what it refuses.

It refuses to trust dashboards.  
It refuses to trust fluent AI.  
It refuses to trust database state without provenance.  
It refuses to trust execution without evidence.  
It refuses to trust closure without receipts.  
It refuses to erase uncertainty just because uncertainty is inconvenient.

That is a rare posture in software.

I decided to cover Minilab because the owner had built a set of rules before he had fully built the product, and the rules were better than most products.

They were simple enough to teach.

Strong enough to constrain machines.

Flexible enough for people.

And strange enough to be original.

> **Definindo um prompt para normalizar nomes de arquivos e pastas**
>
> A questão parece ser sobre a normalização de nomes de arquivos e pastas. Caso o pedido seja gerar um prompt para normalizar estruturas de pasta, isso pode ser feito diretamente com o contexto visto. No entanto, não há necessidade de um bloco de escrita, já que não é um e-mail ou mensagem de chat.

> Pensado para 11s

Yes — but I’d **not use CamelCase for filenames**.

Use CamelCase / exact casing **inside titles and prose** for names like:

```txt
LogLine
Minilab
ChatGPT
LAB512
Hermes
Supabase
PR-001
```

But for filesystem names, use boring, durable, portable names:

```txt
lowercase-kebab-case.md
```

No spaces, no accents, no parentheses, no duplicate counters, no mixed casing unless the filename is a code artifact that truly requires it.

The structure in your screenshot wants to become:

```txt
docs.minilab.work/
  constitutional-stack/
    01-minilab-constitution-strategic-architecture.md
    02-characters-ontology-canon.md
    03-authority-power-doctrine.md

The PDF should be treated as an export, not the canonical source:

```txt
08-minilab-process-catalog.md   canonical
08-minilab-process-catalog.pdf  generated/exported artifact
```

Here’s the prompt I’d use to normalize it:

```txt
You are normalizing the Minilab documentation library.

Context:
Minilab has two main documentation layers plus implementation specs.

Layer 1 is the constitutional stack:
It defines what Minilab is, its ontology, authority, evidence, database projection, config boundaries, App Park doctrine, build equation, solved machinery doctrine, Santo André research practice, and the Minilab LogLine Practice Profile.

Layer 2 is the operating library:
It defines how Minilab operates daily: founding act, organs, source map, LLM offices, morning state, current state, ghost registry, process catalog, vertical runtime doctrine, and Hermes dispatch/execution contract.

Implementation specs are not parent canon. They live under implementation/ and describe concrete substrates such as mini-llms.rs, LAB512, DB runbooks, and runtime adapters.

Task:
Normalize the folder and file names only. Do not rewrite document content unless asked separately.

Rules:
1. Use lowercase-kebab-case for all filenames and folders.
2. Preserve numeric ordering prefixes.
3. Do not use spaces, accents, parentheses, duplicate copy suffixes, or Finder-style counters.
4. Do not use CamelCase in filenames, except when the term is unavoidable in a code identifier. Prefer lowercase-kebab-case even for branded terms.
5. Preserve canonical spelling inside document titles and prose: LogLine, Minilab, ChatGPT, Hermes, LAB512, Supabase, PR-000, PR-001.
6. Deduplicate duplicate documents by keeping the most current/canonical version and moving older copies to archive/duplicates/ only if needed.
7. Markdown files are canonical source. PDFs are generated/export artifacts and should not replace canonical markdown.
8. Do not change semantic numbering without explicit approval.
9. Do not move implementation specs into canon folders.
10. Do not treat current-state/bootstrap documents as universal canon. Keep them in operating-library.

Target structure:

docs.minilab.work/
  constitutional-stack/
    01-minilab-constitution-strategic-architecture.md
    02-characters-ontology-canon.md
    03-authority-power-doctrine.md
    04-evidence-receipt-doctrine.md
    05-dan-control-plane-doctrine.md
    06-database-projection-canon.md
    07-doppler-config-canon.md
    08-app-park-primordial-packet.md
    09-pr-000-build-equation.md
    10-pr-000-solved-machinery-gates-decide-seam.md
    11-santo-andre-lab-modus-operandi.md
    12-minilab-logline-practice-profile-decisions.md

  operating-library/
    01-minilab-founding-act.md
    02-organs-and-jurisdictions.md
    03-source-of-truth-map.md
    04-llm-offices.md
    05-morning-state-ritual.md
    06-current-state-v0.md
    07-ghost-registry-db-projection.md
    08-minilab-process-catalog.md
    09-vertical-runtime-doctrine.md
    10-hermes-dispatch-execution-contract.md

  implementation/
    mini-llms/
      mini-llms-rs-math-contract-v0.1.md
      mini-llms-rs-spec-v0.5.md
      pr-001-lab512-logline-db-runbook-v0.1.md

Optional:
  exports/
    pdf/
      08-minilab-process-catalog.pdf

After normalization, produce:
1. A before → after rename map.
2. A list of detected duplicates.
3. A list of files whose canonical source is unclear.
4. A list of generated/export files.
5. No content edits.
```

So: **CamelCase is fine for concepts, bad for this filesystem.** The filesystem should be dull. The doctrine can be beautiful.

```txt
number + short canonical noun phrase
```

# 01 — Minilab Constitution / Strategic Architecture

Filename does not need to carry the whole title.

## Response:
5/26/2026, 11:44:35 PM

Yes — **conceptually they belong in the same domain**, but I would not put them in the same *layer*.

They are both about LLMs, but they answer different constitutional questions:

```txt
04-offices.md
  = institutional role doctrine

```

So I’d group them under a shared top-level area, but preserve the layer boundary.

Better structure:

```txt
docs.minilab.work/
  constitutional-stack/
    01-constitution.md
    02-ontology.md
    03-authority.md
    04-evidence.md
    05-control-plane.md
    06-database.md
    07-config.md
    08-app-park.md
    09-build-equation.md
    10-solved-machinery.md
    11-santo-andre-lab.md
    12-practice-profile.md

    01-founding-act.md
    02-organs.md
    03-sources.md
    04-offices.md
    05-morning-state.md
    06-current-state.md
    07-ghost-registry.md
    08-process-catalog.md
    09-vertical-runtime.md
    10-hermes-dispatch.md

  llm/
    01-offices.md
    02-mini-llms-spec.md
    03-mini-llms-math-contract.md
    04-lab512-runbook.md
```

But there’s a catch: if you move `04-offices.md` out of `operating-library`, the operating sequence loses a numbered spine. So I’d do one of these two options.

## Option A — Keep spine pure, duplicate by index

Best for canon clarity:

```txt
operating-library/
  04-offices.md

llm/
  01-offices.md -> ../operating-library/04-offices.md
  02-mini-llms-spec.md
  03-mini-llms-math-contract.md
  04-lab512-runbook.md
```

Meaning: `offices` remains part of the operating library, but the LLM shelf links to it.

## Option B — Move all LLM docs into `llm/`

Best for browsing:

```txt
operating-library/
  01-founding-act.md
  02-organs.md
  03-sources.md
  04-morning-state.md
  05-current-state.md
  06-ghost-registry.md
  07-process-catalog.md
  08-vertical-runtime.md
  09-hermes-dispatch.md

llm/
  01-offices.md
  02-mini-llms-spec.md
  03-mini-llms-math-contract.md
  04-lab512-runbook.md
```

I like **Option A** more. Why? Because `LLM Offices` is not implementation. It is operating doctrine. `mini-llms.rs` is implementation/substrate. They should be discoverable together, but not flattened into one category.

Final recommendation:

```txt
Keep 04-offices.md in operating-library.
Create llm/ as a thematic shelf.
Link or copy-index offices there, but do not demote it to implementation.
```

The clean mental model:

```txt
Offices = how LLMs may serve the institution.
mini-llms = how local models metabolize material.
LAB512 runbook = how one runtime proves contact.
```

## Prompt:
5/26/2026, 11:47:06 PM

minillms are an app - though they could be an organ too... hard to tell

## Response:
5/26/2026, 11:47:08 PM

Yeah — this is a real boundary issue. I’d resolve it like this:

```txt id="s52gk5"
mini-llms.rs is not an organ.
mini-llms.rs is an app / runtime substrate.

Local Cognition may be an organ.
mini-llms.rs can be the first implementation of that organ.
```

That preserves the ontology.

An **organ** is permanent institutional function. An **app** is replaceable machinery that serves a function.

So:

```txt id="gvb4ib"
Organ:
  Local Cognition / Intelligence Metabolism

App:
  mini-llms.rs

Runtime:
  lab512-mistral-adapter
  future local model runners

Projection:
  mini_llms.runtime_observations
  mini_llms.logline_candidates
  mini_llms.findings
  mini_llms.ghosts
```

The distinction matters because tomorrow `mini-llms.rs` could be replaced by:

```txt id="53knck"
mini-llms-go
hermes-cognition
a hosted model gateway
a local llama.cpp runner
a different Rust crate
```

But the **institutional need** remains:

```txt id="grk71i"
turn material into candidate structure,
observe model behavior,
detect ghosts,
suggest probes,
evaluate profile health,
without becoming authority.
```

That need is organ-like.

## My naming recommendation

Do not create a top-level folder called only `llm/`. It’s too implementation-colored and too narrow.

Use:

```txt id="ckb168"
cognition/
```

or:

```txt id="jm7d8z"
local-cognition/
```

Best:

```txt id="scpo4u"
cognition/
```

Then structure it like:

```txt id="wbx0ib"
docs.minilab.work/
  operating-library/
    04-offices.md

  cognition/
    01-local-cognition-organ.md
    02-mini-llms-app.md
    03-mini-llms-math.md
    04-lab512-runbook.md
```

But if you do not want to invent a new doc yet:

```txt id="p6d4oc"
cognition/
  01-offices.md              // link/reference to operating-library/04
  02-mini-llms-spec.md
  03-mini-llms-math-contract.md
  04-lab512-runbook.md
```

## The clean final taxonomy

```txt id="uos1mj"
LLM Offices:
  doctrine for how model sessions occupy institutional roles

Local Cognition:
  organ/function of the institution

mini-llms.rs:
  app/runtime substrate serving Local Cognition

LAB512 Runbook:
  implementation probe for one local runtime

Math Contract:
  metabolism metrics for the cognition app
```

So I’d say:

```txt id="lrxe9m"
mini-llms are an app.
The organ is Local Cognition.
```

And in the folder, I’d make that visible:

```txt id="ro4kpg"
cognition/
  00-readme.md
  01-offices.md
  02-mini-llms-app.md
  03-metabolism-math.md
  04-lab512-runtime-runbook.md
```

The README would say:

```txt id="zcgjo5"
This shelf covers Minilab’s cognition layer.

Offices define how LLMs may occupy institutional roles.
mini-llms.rs is the first app/runtime substrate for local cognition.
The math contract measures cognition metabolism.
The LAB512 runbook defines one concrete runtime contact path.

The app is replaceable.
The organ is not.
```

That’s the clean line.

> Pensado para 17s

Yes - **Offices are not an organ**.

The clean taxonomy is:

```txt
Organ = permanent institutional function.
Office = stable role/desk inside an organ.
Model/app/runtime = temporary occupant or tool.
```

That matches the existing doctrine: `02 - Organs and Jurisdictions` says an organ is a permanent institutional function, not a folder, app, schema, chat, or tool; tools may change, organs remain. `02_organs_and_jurisdictions.md` And `04 - LLM Offices` says the office persists while the model is only a temporary occupant. `04_llm_offices.md`

So:

```txt
LLM Offices are not an organ.
They are role definitions across organs.

Morning Scribe belongs to Cockpit.
Ghost Keeper belongs to Evidence / Receipts / Ghosts.
Research Registrar belongs to Santo André Laboratory.
Authority Clerk belongs to Authority.
LAB Reporter belongs to LAB.
Canon Librarian belongs to Constitution.
```

## Should we add Local Cognition as an organ?

I think yes - but carefully.

The original 02 has **nine founding organs**. We can keep those as founding organs and add:

```txt
10. Local Cognition
```

as a **post-founding organ / practice-profile organ**, not pretend it was in the first founding list.

Why it deserves organ status:

```txt
Local Cognition is a permanent institutional function:
turning material into candidates, observations, findings, ghosts, probes, and evaluations
without becoming authority, execution, or closure.
```

mini-llms.rs is not the organ. It is the first app/runtime serving this organ.

```txt
Organ: Local Cognition
App/runtime substrate: mini-llms.rs
Concrete runtime/probe: LAB512 adapter
Database projections: mini_llms.*
```

## Patch I’d add to `02-organs.md`

Add after the nine founding organs section:

```md
## 3.1 Post-Founding Organ: Local Cognition

Local Cognition may be added as a post-founding organ of Minilab.

It governs the institutional use of local/private model cognition below operators.

Local Cognition is not Authority.
Local Cognition is not Cockpit.
Local Cognition is not LAB.
Local Cognition is not Evidence.
Local Cognition is not mini-llms.rs.

Local Cognition is the permanent function that metabolizes material into candidate structure.

mini-llms.rs is the first app/runtime substrate serving this organ.
LAB512 is one possible execution place for this substrate.
Model outputs remain candidates.
Admission, power, execution, and closure remain with their proper organs.
```

Then add a full organ section:

```md
# Organ: Local Cognition

## Mandate

Local Cognition governs how Minilab uses local/private models to metabolize material into LogLine candidates, runtime observations, findings, ghosts, probe suggestions, decision requests, and evaluation records.

It exists so model cognition can be useful without becoming institutional authority.

## Governs

- local/private inference;
- LogLineCandidate extraction;
- RuntimeObservation records;
- model profile evaluation;
- cognition metabolism metrics;
- finding projections;
- ghost candidate projections;
- probe suggestions;
- decision request candidates;
- local model drift and calibration.

## May

- classify material;
- summarize sources;
- extract LogLineCandidate structures;
- record RuntimeObservation;
- project findings and ghost candidates;
- suggest probes;
- suggest decision requests;
- evaluate model profiles;
- expose reasoning-only, parse failure, schema failure, and guard rejection states.

## May Not

- admit acts;
- authorize power;
- execute protected actions;
- dispatch Hermes workorders by itself;
- close receipts;
- promote canon;
- treat model output as evidence;
- treat confidence as authority;
- treat local inference as truth.

## Primary Sources

- mini-llms.rs Spec;
- mini-llms.rs Math Contract;
- PR-001 LAB512 LogLine DB Runbook;
- LLM Offices;
- Vertical Runtime Doctrine;
- Minilab LogLine Practice Profile;
- runtime observations;
- evaluation records.

## Outputs

- LogLineCandidate;
- RuntimeObservation;
- Finding candidate;
- GhostCandidate;
- ProbeSuggestion;
- DecisionRequest;
- profile evaluation;
- metabolism report;
- drift ghost.

## Ghosts

- model output overclaim;
- reasoning-only-no-content;
- parse failure;
- schema failure;
- guard rejected candidate;
- profile health unmeasured;
- calibration missing;
- false closure risk;
- runtime adapter unverified.

## Possible Tool Jurisdictions

- Repo/app: `mini-llms.rs`
- Future app surface: `Local Cognition`
- Future data projection: `mini_llms.*`
- LAB runtime: `LAB512`
```

## Patch to `04-offices.md`

```md
# Office: Cognition Steward

## Organ

Local Cognition

## Mandate

Operate local/private cognition without letting model output become authority, truth, execution, or closure.

## Scope

mini-llms.rs, local model calls, LogLineCandidate extraction, RuntimeObservation records, findings, ghost candidates, probe suggestions, profile evaluation, and drift monitoring.

## Primary Sources

- MINI-LLMS-RS-SPEC
- MINI-LLMS-RS-MATH-CONTRACT
- PR-001-LAB512-RUNBOOK
- runtime observations
- profile evaluations
- Vertical Runtime Doctrine

## May

- run or request local cognition jobs through admitted paths;
- summarize runtime observations;
- classify extraction status;
- project findings and ghost candidates;
- detect false closure risk;
- recommend profile promotion only as candidate;
- name model/runtime ghosts.

## May Not

- admit LogLine Acts;
- authorize execution;
- close receipts;
- treat model output as evidence;
- promote model profiles as authority;
- bypass Hermes or Authority;
- hide reasoning-only/no-content states.

## Output

```txt
Cognition Report

Runtime:
Model:
Input scope:
Output contract:
Extraction status:
Candidate emitted:
Guard status:
Evidence state:
Findings:
Ghosts:
Next probe:
Promotion candidate:
```
```

Good call. Don’t disturb the library just to satisfy taxonomy anxiety.

The current structure already has the important separation:

```txt
Offices = operating doctrine
mini-llms = implementation/app substrate
```

That is enough.

A future `Local Cognition` organ can remain a ghost or later promotion candidate. No need to force it now. The stronger move is to keep the library stable and let usage prove whether the category deserves a new organ.

So current decision:

```txt
Do not reorganize.
Do not add Local Cognition yet.
Keep Offices where they are.
Keep mini-llms as implementation/app docs.
Carry “Local Cognition as possible organ” as a future ghost.
```

Nice restraint. That is very Minilab.

The previous framing made Minilab too central. Your correction separates the public protocol mission from your private operating infrastructure.

The architecture should be:

LogLine Foundation
  simple canon
  parent grammar
  protocol spine
  public legitimacy
LogLine Developer Community

  adapters
  SDKs
  examples

  implementations

  templates, manifests, projectors, receipts, ghosts, probes
  a practical way to instantiate LogLine in the world

  first LogLine Lab
  studies uses and misuses of the technology
  turns claims into invariants, probes, observations, receipts
minilab.work
  Dan Voulez’s personal infrastructure
  not the public protocol
  not the Foundation
  not the community
  a private/operational deployment surface

So the public ladder is:

Foundation → Developer Community → LogLine Lab → Society

And the personal ladder is:

Dan → minilab.work → Santo André Laboratory → experiments/receipts/learning

That is much better.

The objective is not “make Minilab famous.”
The objective is:

LogLine reaches society structurally.

Meaning: not as a consumer app, not as a viral interface, not as “yet another AI tool,” but as a structural protocol that other people, labs, communities, institutions, and systems can instantiate.

And yes: the general public will be late. Protocol adoption is slower than product adoption. People adopt products when they solve visible pain. They adopt protocols when a whole ecosystem slowly realizes the protocol gives them a new category of structural power.

So the near-term audience is not the general public.

It is:

developers
operators
researchers
protocol people
AI safety/accountability builders
labs
small institutions
governance hackers
infra-minded founders

The LogLine Lab becomes the bridge: not “read the theory for two years,” but:

Clone the lab kit.
Declare a practice profile.
Run probes.
Emit receipts.
Keep ghosts.
Study consequences.

Santo André Laboratory then becomes the reference implementation of the method. That aligns with its own doctrine: it is where philosophy is forced to produce probes, and where discoveries must move through invariant → probe → runtime observation → receipt before promotion.  

This also reframes the uploaded “Minilab Practice Profile Decisions” file. The useful content remains, but the institutional naming should probably shift:

Old:
  Minilab Practice Profile
Better:
  LogLine Lab Practice Profile
  or Santo André Lab Practice Profile
  or Dan/minilab.work Local Overlay

Because the file’s own core distinction is still right: parent canon unchanged, local/institutional practice declared separately.  

The final taxonomy:

Foundation is canon.
Community is adoption.
Lab is kit.
Santo André is first lab.
minilab.work is Dan’s infrastructure.
Society is the long horizon.

That’s it. Clean. No mythological blob. No accidental empire under “Minilab.”

## The formal decision

```txt id="cs7r2x"
Decision:
Minilab will no longer be framed as the central public vehicle for LogLine adoption.

LogLine Foundation remains the public canon.
The LogLine Developer Community becomes the builder/adoption layer.
LogLine Lab becomes the reusable quick-start institutional kit.
Santo André Laboratory becomes the first reference LogLine Lab.
minilab.work remains Dan’s private operational infrastructure and local overlay.

and prevents Minilab from becoming a mythological blob.

Consequence:
Rename “Minilab Practice Profile” materials that are reusable into “LogLine Lab Practice Profile.”
Keep Dan-specific operational material under minilab.work or dan.minilab.work overlay.
```

That’s it. This is the right correction: **Foundation is canon, Community is adoption, Lab is kit, Santo André is first lab, minilab.work is private infra, society is the horizon.**

> Thought for 47s

Yes. Recalibrated. The builder community cannot be “the old developer class, but with AI tools.” That class is mutating hard.

The opportunity for LogLine is not to compete for generic coders. It is to gather the people who now feel the pain of AI-generated motion without accountability.

The new thesis:

```txt
The world no longer lacks code.
The world lacks accountable acts.
```

That is where LogLine can win.

## 1. What is left of “builders”?

The builder did not disappear. The builder split.

There are now at least three groups:

```txt
1. Prompt-driven makers
   They can generate apps, scripts, flows, demos.

2. Supervisory engineers
   They direct, inspect, verify, repair, constrain, integrate, and maintain AI-assisted work.

3. Accountability builders
   They care less about “can it generate code?” and more about:
   what happened,
   who did it,
   which runtime did it,
   what evidence exists,
   what can close,
   what remains ghost.
```

LogLine should not chase group 1 as the core community. They are useful users later, but they are unstable early adopters.

The core community is group 2 and group 3.

That is also where the research points. Stack Overflow’s 2025 survey shows AI use is common, but trust is not: more developers actively distrust AI tool accuracy than trust it, and only around 3% highly trust AI output. Experienced developers are especially cautious. ([survey.stackoverflow.co](https://survey.stackoverflow.co/2025/ai/)) DORA’s 2025 AI-assisted development report says AI acts as an amplifier of existing organizational strengths and weaknesses, not a magic productivity layer by itself. ([dora.dev](https://dora.dev/research/2025/dora-report/)) A 2026 longitudinal study describes the shift toward “supervisory engineering work”: directing, evaluating, and correcting AI output, with less time writing code and more burden around verification. ([arXiv](https://arxiv.org/abs/2605.23135?utm_source=chatgpt.com))

So what is left is not “developer as code typist.”

What remains is:

```txt
builder as supervisor
builder as verifier
builder as runtime operator
builder as system steward
builder as evidence designer
builder as protocol implementer
builder as accountability engineer
```

That is your community.

## 2. Why LogLine matters now

The AI-builder world has a structural problem:

```txt
AI can create motion faster than institutions can account for it.
```

Code appears. Apps appear. Agents run. Pull requests appear. APIs are called. Databases mutate. But the system often cannot answer:

```txt
who did what?
under which runtime?
with what authority?
what evidence came back?
what did this actually close?
what remains unknown?
```

That is the opening.

Researchers are already seeing that AI coding does not uniformly improve real work. A randomized trial with experienced open-source developers working on mature projects found that allowing AI tools increased completion time by 19%, despite developers expecting a speedup. The authors point to context, project maturity, and quality standards as possible contributing factors. ([arXiv](https://arxiv.org/abs/2507.09089?utm_source=chatgpt.com)) Another empirical study on AI agent systems found recurring developer challenges around runtime integration, dependency management, orchestration complexity, and evaluation reliability. ([arXiv](https://arxiv.org/abs/2510.25423?utm_source=chatgpt.com))

So the builder community needs fewer magic demos and more operational grammar.

LogLine’s pitch is not:

```txt
Build faster with AI.
```

It is:

```txt
Build systems where AI, humans, runtimes, databases, and agents can be held to account.
```

That is stronger.

## 3. The builder community’s public identity

Name:

```txt
LogLine Developer Community
```

But internally, think:

```txt
LogLine Builder Community
```

Because this is bigger than programmers.

The community includes:

```txt
protocol implementers
runtime builders
projector authors
database people
AI agent builders
local model operators
DevOps/SRE people
security/compliance builders
researchers
small labs
governance hackers
operators of real institutions
```

The public mission:

```txt
Make LogLine structurally adoptable.
```

Not:

```txt
Make Minilab famous.
```

The ladder:

```txt
LogLine Foundation
→ Developer Community
→ LogLine Lab
→ Society
```

The community is the bridge between the pure grammar and real-world adoption.

## 4. Core message to builders

Do not lead with philosophy. Lead with the pain.

```txt
AI can generate code.
Can your system account for what happened?
```

Then introduce the structure:

```txt
LogLine gives you an act grammar.

A LogLine Lab gives you:
runtime envelopes,
projectors,
receipts,
ghosts,
probes,
workorders,
database projections,
and a way to declare local practice.
```

The community motto:

```txt
We Trust and Build with LogLine.
```

But for builders, make it operational:

```txt
No demo without acts.
No execution without runtime.
No claim without evidence.
No closure without receipt.
No missing proof without ghost.
```

That is a culture.

## 5. What the community should build first

The builder community needs artifacts, not slogans.

### A. `logline.foundation`

Purpose:

```txt
The pure parent grammar.
Small, stable, boring, sacred.
```

It should include:

```txt
nine-slot act grammar
canonical examples
hashing/conformance notes if already canon
minimal validator
invalid examples
spec versioning
```

Do not put Minilab-specific runtime envelopes here unless they are formally accepted upstream.

### B. `logline-lab-kit`

Purpose:

```txt
A quick-start kit for operating a laboratory with LogLine.
```

This is where the practical rules live:

```txt
runtime envelopes
practice manifests
projectors
receipt templates
ghost registry
probe templates
database projection schema
sample RLS helpers
sample runtime registry
```

This is not Foundation. This is the first public “use LogLine in the world” kit.

### C. `santo-andre-lab`

Purpose:

```txt
The first reference LogLine Lab.
```

It should show:

```txt
real experiments
misuse studies
invariant → probe → observation → receipt
ghosts carried honestly
practice notes
failures
```

This is where credibility accumulates.

### D. `logline-conformance`

Purpose:

```txt
Let builders know whether they are still speaking LogLine.
```

It should include:

```txt
valid act fixtures
invalid act fixtures
hash vectors
runtime-envelope optional profile tests
projector conformance examples
receipt/ghost examples as practice-level tests
```

### E. `logline-projectors`

Purpose:

```txt
Show how acts become worlds.
```

First projectors:

```txt
registry.entities
registry.runtimes
registry.visas
registry.auth_bindings
audit.mobile_state
evidence.records
receipts.index
ghosts.registry
```

This is where developers will “get it.”

## 6. What the community should *not* be

Do not make it:

```txt
a Discord full of prompt hacks
an AI wrapper cult
a personal Minilab fan club
a Rust-only priesthood
a speculative protocol DAO
a place where screenshots count as proof
a “ship fast with agents” hype room
```

The community must be emotionally open but epistemically strict.

The norm:

```txt
Friendly to builders.
Hostile to unsupported claims.
```

## 7. The ideal early builder personas

### 1. The burned senior engineer

They have seen AI-generated code create hidden debt. They distrust tool output but still use it. They want a way to supervise and prove.

Message:

```txt
LogLine gives your AI-assisted work an accountability spine.
```

### 2. The agent builder drowning in orchestration

They are building with LangChain, LangGraph, CrewAI, Ollama, MCP, Supabase, Redis, pgvector, etc. They know agent systems need memory, tools, permissions, and observability. Stack Overflow’s 2025 survey shows developers building agents are using existing tools like Redis, Supabase, pgvector, Grafana/Prometheus, and Sentry rather than one unified native stack. ([survey.stackoverflow.co](https://survey.stackoverflow.co/2025/ai/))

Message:

```txt
LogLine gives your agents acts, runtime envelopes, visas, receipts, and ghosts.
```

### 3. The local AI operator

They run Ollama, llama.cpp, vLLM, Mistral, local GPUs, private inference, or internal models.

Message:

```txt
LogLine lets local cognition produce candidates without becoming authority.
```

### 4. The open-source maintainer

They need contribution quality, conformance, review, provenance, and less AI slop.

Message:

```txt
Every contribution can carry acts, evidence, and scoped closure.
```

### 5. The governance/security person

They care about auditability, risk, privacy, access, and evidence.

Message:

```txt
LogLine turns permissions, runtime claims, and closure into inspectable acts.
```

### 6. The domain operator

They run a clinic, lab, school, studio, shop, farm, research group, or small company.

Message:

```txt
You do not need to become a software company. You can declare a LogLine Lab.
```

## 8. Community onboarding ladder

Do not ask people to understand the whole system at once.

Give them a ladder.

### Level 0 - Reader

Goal:

```txt
Understand the sentence:
Everything important is a LogLine Act.
```

Artifacts:

```txt
one-page explainer
10 valid acts
10 invalid acts
“database is projection” note
```

Success:

```txt
They can explain act vs projection.
```

### Level 1 - Runner

Goal:

```txt
Run the lab kit locally.
```

Artifacts:

```txt
starter repo
SQLite/Postgres option
sample act log
sample projections
sample ghost registry
```

Success:

```txt
They emit an act and see it projected.
```

### Level 2 - Projector Builder

Goal:

```txt
Write a projector over acts.
```

Examples:

```txt
passport projector
visa projector
receipt projector
inventory projector
workorder projector
```

Success:

```txt
They can show how one act becomes one read model.
```

### Level 3 - Runtime Builder

Goal:

```txt
Create a runtime that emits enveloped acts.
```

Examples:

```txt
shell runtime
HTTP probe runtime
LLM runtime
Hermes worker runtime
Supabase projection runtime
```

Success:

```txt
Runtime emits acts with runtime_id, hashes, and observation metadata.
```

### Level 4 - Lab Steward

Goal:

```txt
Declare a local practice profile.
```

Artifacts:

```txt
manifest
runtimes
projectors
offices
ghost policy
receipt policy
```

Success:

```txt
They can run a small institution from acts.
```

### Level 5 - Foundation Contributor

Goal:

```txt
Propose changes upstream only when practice proves generality.
```

Artifacts:

```txt
LIP / RFC
evidence
implementations
conformance vectors
receipts
ghosts
```

Success:

```txt
They know the difference between Foundation canon and local practice.
```

## 9. Community rituals

This is crucial. Communities are not docs. They are repeated behavior.

### Weekly: Probe Night

Builders bring one claim and turn it into:

```txt
invariant
probe
runtime observation
receipt or ghost
```

### Weekly: Ghost Review

People present what could not close.

Rule:

```txt
A good ghost is not embarrassment.
A ghost is intellectual hygiene.
```

### Biweekly: Projector Clinic

People build or review projections.

Examples:

```txt
How does this act become a visa?
How does this act become stock movement?
How does this runtime result become evidence?
```

### Monthly: Runtime Demo Day

Only demos with:

```txt
act log
runtime envelope
projection
evidence
ghosts
```

No raw “look at this app” demos.

### Monthly: Conformance Day

People run validators, fix fixtures, add invalid cases.

### Quarterly: Lab Reports

Each lab publishes:

```txt
what loaded
what was configured
what was declared
what ran
what closed
what stayed ghost
```

## 10. Contribution categories

Make it easy to contribute without becoming a core theorist.

```txt
docs:
  examples, diagrams, explainers

fixtures:
  valid/invalid acts

projectors:
  SQL, Rust, TypeScript, Python projectors

runtimes:
  shell, HTTP, LLM, database, Hermes, local model

adapters:
  Supabase, Postgres, SQLite, GitHub, MCP, Ollama

receipts:
  scoped closure examples

ghosts:
  named failure/missing-proof patterns

labs:
  local practice profiles

conformance:
  validators, test vectors, CI

education:
  tutorials, workshops, videos
```

This matters because the “builder community” now includes people who can use AI to produce code, but need strong contribution slots. The community should make the valuable work legible.

## 11. The first public repos

I would start with five repos, not twenty.

```txt
logline-foundation
logline-conformance
logline-lab-kit
logline-projectors
santo-andre-lab
```

Later:

```txt
logline-runtimes
logline-hermes
logline-supabase
logline-js
logline-rs
logline-py
```

Keep the first wave painfully clear.

## 12. First 90-day plan

### Days 0-14: Stabilize the story

Ship:

```txt
public architecture page
Foundation vs Lab vs Local Overlay explanation
glossary
10 canonical examples
README: “What is left of building after AI?”
```

Core public message:

```txt
AI generates motion.
LogLine gives motion accountability.
```

### Days 15-30: Release the starter

Ship:

```txt
logline-lab-kit alpha
one local database setup
sample acts
sample runtime envelope
sample projectors
sample receipt
sample ghost
```

Goal:

```txt
A builder can run one command and see act → projection.
```

### Days 31-60: Recruit first 20 builders

Do not recruit masses.

Recruit manually:

```txt
5 senior engineers
5 AI agent builders
3 infra/SRE/security people
3 local AI operators
2 open-source maintainers
2 domain operators
```

Give them a clear ask:

```txt
Run the kit.
Create one projector or runtime.
Publish one ghost.
```

### Days 61-90: Produce public proof

Ship:

```txt
first community lab reports
first external projector
first external runtime
first conformance contribution
first receipt review session
first “ghosts we carried” post
```

The goal is not growth. The goal is credibility.

## 13. Six-month plan

By six months, the community should have:

```txt
3-5 independent LogLine Labs
10+ projectors
5+ runtimes/adapters
50+ conformance fixtures
public ghost registry patterns
receipt examples across domains
one monthly community call
one strong “Applied LogLine” essay
```

The public claim:

```txt
LogLine is not just Dan’s system.
Other people can instantiate it.
```

## 14. One-year plan

By one year:

```txt
LogLine Foundation stable enough for outsiders
LogLine Lab Kit usable by serious builders
Santo André Laboratory publishing reference studies
Community maintaining projectors/adapters
At least one non-Dan lab producing receipts/ghosts
Conformance suite used in CI
```

This is the moment where LogLine can start approaching society structurally.

Not through a consumer app.

Through repeatable labs.

## 15. What to say to builders

Use this:

```txt
You do not need another AI coding assistant.

You need a way to know what your assistants, runtimes, databases, workers, and humans actually did.

LogLine gives you the act.
LogLine Lab gives you the practice.
Your local overlay gives you your institution.
```

And this:

```txt
If AI turns everyone into a builder,
then accountability becomes the scarce skill.
```

That is probably the sharpest public line.

## 16. Community rules

Make these explicit:

```txt
1. Acts before stories.
2. Runtime before execution claims.
3. Evidence before receipts.
4. Ghosts before fake certainty.
5. Projections are not truth.
6. AI output is candidate, not authority.
7. Local practice is declared, not smuggled into Foundation.
8. Foundation changes require evidence from practice.
```

This will repel some people. Good.

It will attract the right ones.

## 17. Risks

### Risk: too abstract

Mitigation:

```txt
Lead with runnable lab kit, not theory.
```

### Risk: too Dan-centered

Mitigation:

```txt
Foundation → Community → LogLine Lab → Society.
minilab.work stays private/local.
```

### Risk: AI-hype contamination

Mitigation:

```txt
No demos without acts/evidence/ghosts.
```

### Risk: too much doctrine

Mitigation:

```txt
Every doctrine page must have one runnable example.
```

### Risk: builder fatigue

Mitigation:

```txt
Good first issues must be projectors, fixtures, adapters, examples - not philosophy.
```

### Risk: Foundation instability

Mitigation:

```txt
Keep Foundation tiny.
Move operational rules into LogLine Lab profile.
```

## 18. The real answer: what is left?

What is left of builders is not the California genius myth.

What is left is more interesting:

```txt
people who can make generated systems accountable;
people who can turn AI output into admitted acts;
people who can build projectors instead of dashboards;
people who can operate runtimes without worshiping them;
people who can preserve ghosts instead of selling certainty;
people who can make small institutions legible.
```

That is the LogLine builder community.

And the public invitation is simple:

```txt
Start with LogLine.
Configure a Lab.
Declare your practice.
Build with receipts.
Carry your ghosts.
```

This is not nostalgic developer culture. It is the next one.

> Thought for 28s

Yes. **LogLine Lab with Santo André as the face** is the right move - but the framing has to be precise:

```txt
LogLine Foundation = canon
LogLine Developer Community = builders
LogLine Lab = replicable kit / public method
Santo André Laboratory = face, reference lab, and proof theater
minilab.work = Dan’s private operating deployment
```

That gives the project a public front without making Dan’s private infrastructure carry the whole protocol mythology. Very good correction.

## The core positioning

**LogLine Lab** should be the thing people can clone, study, adapt, and instantiate.

**Santo André Laboratory** should be the first named lab, the one that shows the attitude:

```txt
We do not ship vibes.
We turn claims into probes.
We keep ghosts.
We close narrowly.
We publish what survived.
```

That matches the Santo André doctrine exactly. The doc defines Santo André as a “receipted research institution” whose job is to discover structures hidden in executable doctrine, turn them into falsifiable invariants, test them against runtime/evidence, and promote only scoped, receipted discoveries. Its sharper sentence is already excellent: **“Santo André Laboratory is where philosophy is forced to produce probes.”** `santo-andre-lab-modus-operandi-doc-11-confirmed-by-witness-patch-v2.md`

So the split is:

```txt
LogLine Lab:
  the method and kit

Santo André Laboratory:
  the face, tone, first implementation, research theater, public proof loop
```

Not “Santo André is the product.” More like:

```txt
Santo André is the first lab coat.
```

## Why this matches the current world

The current world is almost begging for this. AI has made building cheaper, but verification, accountability, and institutional memory are now the scarce resources.

Stack Overflow’s 2025 survey shows developers are using AI tools but trust is strained: more developers actively distrust AI accuracy than trust it, and only a tiny fraction report high trust in AI output. ([Stack Overflow Insights](https://survey.stackoverflow.co/2025/ai/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)) DORA’s 2025 report frames AI as an amplifier: it magnifies the strengths of already-healthy organizations and the dysfunctions of struggling ones, instead of magically creating engineering maturity. ([Dora](https://dora.dev/dora-report-2025/?utm_source=chatgpt.com))

That is the opening for LogLine Lab:

```txt
AI made everyone capable of producing motion.
LogLine Lab teaches people how to account for motion.
```

And the agent world reinforces it. The World Economic Forum says many executives plan to adopt AI agents soon, while the gap between experimentation and mature oversight is widening, with risks around autonomy, safety, integration, and trust. ([World Economic Forum](https://www.weforum.org/publications/ai-agents-in-action-foundations-for-evaluation-and-governance/?utm_source=chatgpt.com)) Research on AI agent systems points to production needs like observability, audit logs, incident paths, and governance when agents use tools with side effects. ([arXiv](https://arxiv.org/html/2601.01743v1?utm_source=chatgpt.com))

So the current world vision is not “teach people another framework.”

It is:

```txt
Give the AI age a lab discipline.
```

## The public promise

I would position LogLine Lab like this:

```txt
LogLine Lab is a quick-start kit for people who want to operate with LogLine:

load the grammar,
declare a practice,
register runtimes,
run probes,
emit evidence,
close receipts,
carry ghosts,
and learn in public.
```

Santo André Laboratory becomes the first reference implementation of that method:

```txt
Santo André Laboratory is the first public LogLine Lab:
a place where claims are converted into invariants,
invariants into probes,
probes into observations,
observations into receipts,
and unresolved claims into ghosts.
```

That last bit is the face. It has attitude. It has a method. It has humor without being unserious.

## The tone

The tone should not be academic-cold, and it should not be startup-peppy.

It should be:

```txt
warmly severe
slightly funny
anti-hype
pro-builder
pro-receipt
ghost-friendly
```

Like:

```txt
Bring your theory.
We’ll give it a helmet.

Bring your agent.
We’ll ask what it actually did.

Bring your demo.
We’ll look for the receipt.

Bring your certainty.
We have a ghost registry.
```

That is the Santo André voice. Serious enough for protocol people, funny enough for humans.

## The “current world” narrative

The world is full of:

```txt
vibe-coded apps
agent demos
autonomous workflows
AI copilots
tool-calling chains
dashboards
observability stacks
compliance theater
founders shipping things nobody can explain two weeks later
```

Santo André says:

```txt
Cute. Where is the act?
```

That is the humor.

Not mockery. Discipline with a raised eyebrow.

## The public hierarchy

I’d write the hierarchy this way:

```txt
LogLine Foundation
  The grammar.

LogLine Developer Community
  The people building validators, runtimes, projectors, SDKs, examples, conformance suites.

LogLine Lab
  The practical kit: manifests, runtime envelopes, projectors, probes, receipts, ghosts.

Santo André Laboratory
  The first reference lab and public face of the method.

Society
  The long horizon: institutions that can act, prove, remember, and admit uncertainty.
```

Santo André is not above the community. It is the **flagship lab**.

```txt
Foundation defines.
Community builds.
Lab instantiates.
Santo André demonstrates.
Society adopts.
```

## What Santo André should publish

The Santo André doc already has the right artifact set: experiments, discoveries, invariants, probes, evidence, receipts, ghosts, governance drafts. `santo-andre-lab-modus-operandi-doc-11-confirmed-by-witness-patch-v2.md` That should become the public content engine.

Publish in formats people can understand:

```txt
1. Experiment notes
   “We tested this invariant.”

2. Ghost reports
   “Here is what we still cannot honestly claim.”

3. Receipt examples
   “This claim closed under this evidence, and only this scope.”

4. Misuse studies
   “Here is how AI systems overclaim.”

5. Runtime autopsies
   “This agent acted. Here is the act graph.”

6. Lab kits
   “Clone this and run your own probe.”
```

The weekly rhythm in the doc is already strong: choose thesis/ghost, formulate invariant, write probe, run probe, capture evidence, then write scoped receipt or decide promotion. `santo-andre-lab-modus-operandi-doc-11-confirmed-by-witness-patch-v2.md` That can become public ritual content.

## Website / landing page structure

I’d make `logline.lab` or `/lab` say:

```txt
LogLine Lab

A practical laboratory kit for building with LogLine.

Not another AI agent framework.
Not another dashboard.
Not another vibe-coded workflow.

A lab discipline:

Acts before stories.
Runtimes before execution claims.
Evidence before receipts.
Ghosts before false certainty.
```

Then:

```txt
Featured Lab:
Santo André Laboratory

Where philosophy is forced to produce probes.
```

And three buttons:

```txt
Start a Lab
Read Santo André Experiments
Join the Builder Community
```

## Humor lines that actually fit

These are usable:

```txt
No receipt, no parade.
```

```txt
Your agent had a feeling. Adorable. Where is the runtime observation?
```

```txt
A ghost is not a bug. It is the system refusing to lie.
```

```txt
We support vibes in the intake form. They do not pass conformance.
```

```txt
Dashboards are mood boards unless backed by acts.
```

```txt
If your demo cannot name what it did, it did not graduate from the Lab.
```

```txt
Receipts beat charisma.
```

That last one is already in the Santo André wall rules. Keep it. It is iconic. `santo-andre-lab-modus-operandi-doc-11-confirmed-by-witness-patch-v2.md`

## The face of Santo André

Santo André should feel less like “research division” and more like a **place**.

A public face:

```txt
Santo André Laboratory
The first LogLine Lab.

A small research institution for executable doctrine,
AI accountability,
runtime evidence,
ghost discipline,
and the study of systems that refuse to lie.
```

Its persona:

```txt
Brazilian, precise, irreverent, receipt-obsessed.
```

Not Silicon Valley cathedral. More like:

```txt
a neighborhood lab with a whiteboard, a coffee stain, a runtime log, and absolutely no patience for fake closure.
```

That is memorable.

## Strategic role

Santo André gives LogLine something most protocols lack: a human place where the method is visibly practiced.

Without Santo André:

```txt
LogLine risks looking like a spec.
```

With Santo André:

```txt
LogLine looks like a practice.
```

And that matters because adoption is social. People do not only adopt specs; they adopt examples, rituals, jokes, artifacts, and the feeling that a group of people knows how to behave.

Santo André supplies that behavior.

## The sharper public thesis

```txt
The AI era does not need more demos.
It needs laboratories.

LogLine Lab is the kit.
Santo André is the first lab.
```

That is the line.

## Final recommendation

Yes: make **LogLine Lab** the public bridge, with **Santo André Laboratory as the face**.

But keep the layers clean:

```txt
LogLine Foundation:
  protocol legitimacy

LogLine Developer Community:
  builder adoption

LogLine Lab:
  replicable method and kit

Santo André Laboratory:
  flagship lab, public proof engine, cultural face

minilab.work:
  Dan’s private deployment
```

The public mood:

```txt
serious protocol
practical lab
warmly skeptical community
ghost-friendly humor
zero tolerance for fake closure
```

And the signature:

```txt
We Trust and Build with LogLine.

Bring claims.
We’ll bring probes.
```

Yes. This is the missing piece: **the Lab must not only be a folder, doctrine, or kit. It must be a clocked organism.**

Not “autonomous agent.” Not “cron chaos.” More like:

```txt id="8kojz3"
a living institution with a pulse,
where every pulse can become a LogLine Act,
